        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b00444"><meta property="og:title" content="Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co2P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/acsami.8b00444.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="The Co2P nanoparticles hybridized with unique N-doping carbon matrices have been successfully designed employing ZIF-67 as the precursor via a facile two-step procedure. The Co2P nanostructures are shielded with reduced graphene oxide (rGO) to enhance electrical conductivity and mitigate volume expansion/shrinkage during sodium storage. As anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the novel architectures of Co2P@N-C@rGO exhibited excellent sodium storage performance with a high reversible capacity of 225 mA h g–1 at 50 mA g–1 after 100 cycles. Our study demonstrates the significant potential of Co2P@N-C@rGO as anode materials for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/acsami.8b00444.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ea1acda9775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ea1acda9775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '6dc3c928-7380-4c1a-b073-d0879379a40c'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ea1acda9775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Metal–Organic Frameworks-Derived Co&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P@N-C@rGO with Dual Protection Layers for Improved Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b00444", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Ontario N6A 5B9, Canada</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Xueliang++Sun" data-id="article_author_search">Xueliang Sun</a></span></div></div></div></span></li></ul><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" issue&nbsp;</span><time datetime="2018-05-02" class="article__history__time">2 May 2018</time></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D14641%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DRan%2BJin%252C%2BXifei%2BLi%252C%2BYuxiu%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D17%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b00444%26title%3DMetal%25E2%2580%2593Organic%2BFrameworks-Derived%2BCo2P%2540N-C%2540rGO%2Bwith%2BDual%2BProtection%2BLayers%2Bfor%2BImproved%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D14648%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2018" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The Co<sub>2</sub>P nanoparticles hybridized with unique N-doping carbon matrices have been successfully designed employing ZIF-67 as the precursor via a facile two-step procedure. The Co<sub>2</sub>P nanostructures are shielded with reduced graphene oxide (rGO) to enhance electrical conductivity and mitigate volume expansion/shrinkage during sodium storage. As anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the novel architectures of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO exhibited excellent sodium storage performance with a high reversible capacity of 225 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. Our study demonstrates the significant potential of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO as anode materials for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D14641%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DRan%2BJin%252C%2BXifei%2BLi%252C%2BYuxiu%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D17%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b00444%26title%3DMetal%25E2%2580%2593Organic%2BFrameworks-Derived%2BCo2P%2540N-C%2540rGO%2Bwith%2BDual%2BProtection%2BLayers%2Bfor%2BImproved%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D14648%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Anode%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Co2p&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Co2P">Co<sub>2</sub>P</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=ZIF-67&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ZIF-67">ZIF-67</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reduced+Graphene+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reduced%20graphene%20oxide">reduced graphene oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20materials">anode materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the depletion of fossil fuels, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely applied in portable devices and electrical vehicles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> However, the limited supply of lithium in the earth heavily restricts the development of LIBs in large-scale energy storage. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn the attention due to the abundant natural supply and low cost of sodium, which is considered one of the best substitutes for LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 2 and 3">(2,3)</a> A variety of anode materials have been found and discussed in detail for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Recently, metal phosphorous (e.g., FeP, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> NiP<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> SnP<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> CuP<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> and cobalt-based phosphide <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a>) have been demonstrated as one of the most promising electrode materials for SIBs, in view of their high theoretical gravimetric/volumetric energy densities and relatively low intercalation potentials. Various cobalt-based phosphides consisting of Co<sub>2</sub>P, CoP, CoP<sub>2</sub>, and CoP<sub>3</sub> were all warmly welcomed by the researchers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a> However, only CoP has been successfully applied in SIBs. For instance, the ball-milling method was used to obtain nanosized CoP particles showing a high initial capacity of 770 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> but a poor cycle performance (only 315 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 25 cycles) in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> In comparison to the other cobalt-based phosphide, the orthorhombic structured Co<sub>2</sub>P shows more Co element and metallic character; as a result, the Co<sub>2</sub>P displays a better structural stability and a higher electrical conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Thus, it was demonstrated that the Co<sub>2</sub>P anode exhibits an excellent electrochemical performance for LIBs; for instance, the Co<sub>2</sub>P/graphene nanocomposites reached a high reversible capacity of 892 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> As a result, it is expected that the Co<sub>2</sub>P anode may show a good prospective application in SIBs. Unfortunately, few researches were reported to focus on the Co<sub>2</sub>P anode for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, for the first time, the cobalt-based metal organic framework (ZIF-67) was utilized as a self-template to obtain the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-doped C (Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C) anode for SIBs. The ZIF-67 has wonderful advantages, such as high porosity, large surface area, and unique positive 12-hedral structure, which is a good choice as a precursor. Furthermore, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C is decorated by reduced graphene oxide (rGO) to fabricate the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO nanocomposites. With the help of dual layers of N-doped carbon and rGO, the nanocomposites deliver an enhanced cycling performance and rate capability in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of ZIF-67</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical synthesis of ZIF-67, Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (249.0 mg) (Tianjin Jinghongxin Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.) and 2-methylimidazole (328.0 mg) (Tianjin Jinghongxin Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.) were dissolved in 25.0 mL methanol (Tianjin Jinghongxin Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.). Then, the two solutions were mixed for 24 h. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The precipitate was collected by centrifugation and washed with methanol for several times. Finally, the products were dried in vacuum at over 80 °C for 24 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Synthesis of Co-P@N-C</h3><div class="NLM_p last">ZIF-67 was heated at 900 °C for 3 h and later naturally cooled to 30 °C under an Ar atmosphere. The carbonized samples (Co@N-C) and NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> (Tianjin Jinghongxin Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.) in porcelain boats were treated at 300 °C for 2 h under the same Ar atmosphere. The final sintering products were labeled as Co-P@N-C.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The graphene oxide (GO) was fabricated as previously reported by us. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> The obtained Co-P@N-C (30 mg) was dissolved in 60 mL butanol (Tianjin Jinghongxin Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.), and the graphene oxide (20 mg) also was disperse in 10 mL ethanediol (Tianjin Jinghongxin Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.) under vigorous ultrasonic stirring. The two solutions were mixed well under stirring. The mixed solution was then putted in a Teflon-lined autoclave and maintained at 200 °C for 24 h. Lastly, the precipitates were washed with ethanol and deionized water and dried at 60 °C for 24 h. The resultant materials were labeled as Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO. For comparison, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C was synthesized via the similar procedures without the additive of rGO, which was labeled as Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Physical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The microstructures of Co@N-C, Co-P@N-C, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO were investigated by an X-ray diffractometer (D8 Advance of Bruker, Germany) with Cu Kα radiation ranging from 20 to 80°. The rGO contents in Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO were confirmed via thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, METTLER TOLEDO TGA/DSC-1) in air over a temperature range of 30–1000 °C. The Raman spectra were performed on LabRAM HR800. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms were obtained using a Quantachrome Autosorb-iQ Automated Gas Sorption System at 77 K (before sorption measurements, the samples were out-gassed in high vacuum at 473 K for 24 h). The morphologies of the samples were observed using field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) of SU8010 and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) of JEM-3000F at an acceleration voltage of 200 kV. The SEM equipped with an energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy of INCA X-sight was used to analyze the element composition. The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was recorded on a VG ESCALAB MK II X-ray photoelectron spectrometer.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Eighty weight percentage active material (e.g., Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO and so on), 10 wt % conductive carbon black (Super-P), and 10 wt % poly(vinylidene difluoride) were dissolved in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidinone and formed a uniform slurry, which was cast onto the copper foil and dried in vacuums at 80 °C for 12 h. Subsequently, the electrodes were punched into the disk with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm and the loading of the electrode was about 0.55 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. CR2032 coin-typed half-cells used to evaluate the electrochemical testing were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The sodium foil was used as the counter and reference electrodes and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixed solvent of ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate with a volume ratio of 1:1 was used as an electrolyte. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were conducted using the battery tester (LANHE CT2001A) in the voltage range from 0.01 to 3.0 V. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) were carried out at a scan rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the same voltage range on an electrochemical workstation (Princeton Applied Research Versa STAT 4). The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was tested via the same electrochemical workstation within the alternating current amplitude of 5.0 mV in the frequency range from 0.01 Hz to 100 kHz. All of the electrochemical characterizations were performed at room temperature.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The formation of ZIF-67 precursor is analyzed by SEM, X-ray diffraction (XRD), and N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>. The size of ZIF-67 is measured about 800 nm, and the monodispersed particles reveal 12 aspects with a smooth surface. All of the diffraction peaks of the synthesized ZIF-67 are similar to previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, the N<sub>2</sub> sorption behavior of ZIF-67 displays typical type I isotherms. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller of ZIF-67 is estimated to be 1358.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, and the pore size is approximately 1.34 nm, which is almost equal to the ZIF-67 cavity size (1.17 nm). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a, b) SEM images; (c) enlarged XRD diffraction peaks; and (d) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and pore-size distributions of ZIF-67. The inset is corresponding pore-size distribution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,b shows the XRD patterns of Co@N-C, Co-P@N-C, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO. As show in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, three big diffraction peaks of Co@N-C are in good accordance with the standard diffraction peaks of Co (JPCDS file no. 89-7093). A small peak at 25.1° corresponds to the C(002) plane, which is in line with the previous literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24−26)</a> Both Co-P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C also consist of this peak, indirectly indicating that the carbon layer on the surface has not been destroyed. The part diffraction peaks of Co-P@N-C at 31.4, 36.2, 46.0, and 48.1° are ascribed to the (011), (111), (112), and (211) planes of CoP (JPCDS file no. 89-2598), respectively, and the diffraction peaks of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C at 40.6, 43.2, 44.1, 51.9, 52.1, 55.8, and 56.6° may be indexed to the (121), (211), (130), (131), (002), (040), and (320) planes of Co<sub>2</sub>P (JPCDS file no. 32-0306). After the further hydrothermal reduction treatment, the blank samples (Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C) show five main planes (40.6, 43.2, 44.1, 51.9, and 55.8°), which all match well with the Co<sub>2</sub>P planes, respectively. The Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO XRD patterns are similar to the blank samples (Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C), indicating that rGO has no influence on the Co<sub>2</sub>P structure and crystal type. Due to the addition of graphene, a new carbon peak (29.5°) corresponds to the (110) plane. The emergence of this new peak weakened the original carbon peak.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns of Co@N-C; (b) XRD patterns of Co-P@N-C, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO; (c) Raman spectra of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO and rGO; and (d) TGA curves of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Raman spectra are examined to characterize Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c. The two peaks of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO and rGO are located at about 1346 and 1583 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, amounting to the D band and G band. As we all know, the D band is rooted in the disordered graphitic carbon and the G band results from sp<sup>2</sup> bonded in carbon-based materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> The level of the graphitic structural disorder can be shown by the intensity ratio of the D to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO and rGO was 1.84 and 2.08, respectively. The decrease in <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> displays the higher graphitization structural degree. The two small peaks are centered at 1450 and 1513 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, resulting from the material itself. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of the synthesized Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO were determined under air flow, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d. The oxidation temperature of the N-doped carbon and rGO is similar, and the content of graphene may not be read out directly. During the TGA measurement in air flow, the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C still remains 58% of the product. It is well known that rGO will react with O<sub>2</sub> at high temperature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> When the temperature gradually rises from room temperature to 1000 °C, both N-C and rGO in Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO may be completely oxidized to 40% of the initial quality. If one thinks that the content of rGO is <i>x</i>%, then the content of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C is (1 – <i>x</i>%) in Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO. The following equation can be obtained: (1 – <i>x</i>%) × 58% = 40%, and the content of rGO in Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO is approximately calculated to be 31.1%, which is almost equal to the theoretical content of rGO in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> shows the morphology characteristics of the synthesized Co@N-C, Co-P@N-C, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO. Obviously, Co@N-C displays an average diameter of around 600 nm with some wrinkles on the surface. The main reason is the decomposition of organic ligands under high pyrolysis temperature. Fortunately, the nanostructures are almost retained. The subsequent phosphonation step and graphene coating may not change the shape of the pristine product. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C consists of multiple Co<sub>2</sub>P nanoparticles encapsulated by the N-doped rough porous carbon matrices, similar to Co@N-C and Co-P@N-C. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c suggests that Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C is wrapped around by a typical rippled rGO. The N-doped rough porous effectively avoids the aggregation of the Co<sub>2</sub>P nanoparticles, and is propitious to the diffusion of electrons and sodium ion. In addition, the rGO coating may mitigate the volume expansion/shrinkage upon cycling, and improves the capacity of the anode materials for SIBs. The elemental mapping further confirmed that Co and P elements were dispersed uniformly throughout the graphene in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of (a<sub>1</sub>–a<sub>3</sub>) Co@N-C, (b<sub>1</sub>–b<sub>3</sub>) Co-P@N-C, and (c<sub>1</sub>–c<sub>3</sub>) Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, the HR-TEM indicates that the CoP and Co<sub>2</sub>P nanoparticles are encapsulated by the N-doped rough porous carbon and rGO matrices. Obviously, the nanoparticles of Co<sub>2</sub>P and CoP have the dimensions of around 120 nm in the primary particulate. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the crystal lattice spacings of 0.375, 0.247, and 0.196 nm correspond to the (101), (111), and (112) plane of CoP, respectively. Furthermore, the crystal lattice spacings (0.221 and 0.208 nm) of Co-P@N-C well match with the (121) and (211) planes of Co<sub>2</sub>P. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, the selected area electron diffraction rings of Co-P@N-C are consistent with (111) and (211) planes of CoP and (130) and (320) planes of Co<sub>2</sub>P. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d,e, the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C is coated by rGO, and the selected area electron diffraction rings can be considered for the (121), (211), and (320) planes of Co<sub>2</sub>P (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. TEM images and selected-area diffraction patterns of (a–c) Co-P@N-C and (d–f) Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Electronic structure and chemical components of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO are characterized using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The full survey in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the existence of C, Co, P, N, and O elements. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The high-resolution P 2p XPS spectra display three peaks in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. One of the two peaks at 129.6 eV (2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 130.6 eV (2p<sub>1/2</sub>) can be assigned to the binding energies (BEs) of P 2p in CoP and Co<sub>2</sub>P, respectively. The BE of the P 2p spectra at 133.8 eV is attributed to oxidized P species, possibly due to the superficial oxidation of CoP. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, two peaks of the high-resolution Co 2p XPS spectra at 781.6 and 778.7 eV are attributed to the existence of Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and another main peak at 793.7 eV well matches with the energy level of Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. The intense satellite peaks centered at 786.6, 797.6, and 802.9 eV may correspond to the shakeup excitation of the high-spin Co<sup>2+</sup> ions. The Co peak at 781.6 eV and the P peak at 129.6 eV are related to Co<sub>2</sub>P and CoP. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the C XPS spectra, which can be split into peaks at 284.9, 286.0, and 289.5 eV corresponding to the C–C, C–N, and O–C═O, respectively. According to the XPS analysis of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO, the molar content of Co is 67.52% and that of P is 32.45% without the influence of C, N, and O. The intensity ratio of Co to P is approximately 1:2 with the formation of Co<sub>2</sub>P.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) XPS survey spectra and (b–d) high-resolution XPS spectrum of P 2p, Co 2p, and C 1s of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Co-P@N-C, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, rGO, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO composite are tested as anode materials for SIBs. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is carried out to investigate the electrochemical behavior of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO at a scan rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the fixed voltage ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V at ambient temperature, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3a</a>. The proposed Na storage mechanism is listed as below, which is based on the electrochemical reactions of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>The Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C show five peaks in the first discharge, attributed to the conversion reactions of Co<sub>2</sub>P, leading to the formation of Co and Na<sub>3</sub>P, as well as the decomposition of electrolyte and formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 and 9">(7,9)</a> In the following discharge cycles, the peaks of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO at 0.01, 0.65, and 0.95 V are ascribed to the formation of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>P. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref35" aria-label="reference 12 and 35">(12,35)</a> During all of the three anodic scans of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO, a strong peak at around 0.08 V, a weak peak at around 0.34 V, and a small peak at around 1.76 V are observed, which corresponded to the desodiation of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>P compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> For Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, the main 1.76 V peak shifts to a broad 2.24 V and the discharge peak is not obvious because the electrochemical activity of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C is relatively low, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The SEM images shows that Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO has the rGO protection, relative to Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C. It is propitious to form a stable SEI and an enhance electrochemical activity. Obviously, the CV curves of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO are almost coincident in the second and third cycles with an excellent reversibility. It clearly indicates that the rGO coating mitigates the volume expansion/shrinkage of Co<sub>2</sub>P anode; as a result, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO reveals a significant advantage for sodium storage compared with Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C. The electrochemical performance of the as-synthesized Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO is evaluated by galvanostatic discharge/charge profiling at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. In agreement with the CV curves, the platform at around 0.01 V in the discharge process can be observed. After the second cycles, their profiles overlap well with each other, showing an excellent reversibility as well.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/medium/am-2018-00444x_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Cyclic voltammograms of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO in the voltage range of 0.01–3 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scanning rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Discharge/charge profiles of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO in the 1st, 3rd, 5th, and 10th cycles. (c) The comparison of cycling performance of Co-P@N-C, rGO, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability of Co-P@N-C, rGO, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO at various current densities. (e) Cycling performance of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO at a high density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/asset/images/large/am-2018-00444x_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b00444&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c compares the cycling response of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO, rGO, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co-P@N-C electrodes in the first 100 cycles at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Co-P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C anode exhibit poor discharge capacities of 93.5 and 120.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, respectively. It is generally documented that the poor performance may be associated with the drastic volume change during the sodiation/desodiation processes. The rGO also shows a low discharge of 139 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. As expected, the initial discharge/charge capacities of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO are 1348.3/336.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which show a low initial coulombic efficiency. It is mainly because of the formation of SEI and electrolyte decomposition. In the following cycles, the Coulombic efficiency increases to around 100%. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 and 9">(7,9)</a> In the following cycles, the Coulombic efficiency quickly increases to around 100%. Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO reveals a decline in capacity in the first few cycles, which can also be clearly observed. As we all know, size expansion of 491% corresponds to the phase transition from P to Na<sub>3</sub>P. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> In the first few cycles, some large particles with the volume expansion produce a breakdown of the SEI film; as a result, both capacity and Coulombic efficiency decrease. Many anode materials exhibited similar trend in the previous literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref5 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref5 ref38" aria-label="reference 2, 5 and 38">(2,5,38)</a> In circulation process, the big particles are cracked into small particles, which are still wrapped by rGO. The rGO may alleviate volume expansion/shrinkage and enhance the electrical conductivity of the Co<sub>2</sub>P anode. Thus, the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO composite electrode shows a relatively stable capacity; for instance, even after 100 cycles, the capacity can be still retained at a value of 225 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d compares the rate capability of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co-P@N-C anodes at varied current densities from 100 to 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It exhibits a specific capacity of 323 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> during the first 10 cycles and then shows reversible capacities of 276, 240, 212, and 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at various current densities of 100, 200, 400, and 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at a high current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, it can still retain a reversible capacity of 170 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which are higher than those of rGO, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C, and Co-P@N-C.  Obviously, when the current density back to&nbsp;50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 70 cycles, the reversible capacity reached 275 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which shows a superior rate capability. Clearly, one can see from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e that Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO exhibits a high specific capacity of 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after continuous cycling for 1000 cycles at a high current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. It is believed that the rGO with a good electrical conductivity and a high surface area is favorable for the rapid transmission of sodium ions. As a result, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO reveals a long cycling performance at high current densities, which is conducive to practical application.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> compares the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of Co-P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO in the 1st, 5th, and 10th cycles. The EIS curves consist of a straight line in the low-frequency range and a semicircle in the high/medium-frequency range. The equivalent circuit used to fit the Nyquist traces consists of the electrolyte resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), the resistance (<i>R</i><sub>sf</sub>), and capacitance (CPE<sub>sf</sub>) of the SEI formed on the electrodes, the resistance of charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), double-layer capacitance (CPE<sub>ct</sub>), and the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> The fitting <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values are compared in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. On one hand, these parameters demonstrate that as expected, the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO electrode shows a lower charge-transfer resistance (78.1 Ω) than the pure Co-P@NC electrode (392.9 Ω) in the first cycle. On the other hand, by observing the changes in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> in the fifth cycles (137.4 Ω) and 10th cycles (156.8 Ω), the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO slightly increases due to the degradation of the electrode caused by volume changes during the charge/discharge process. But the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO is still lower than that of Co-P@N-C. The addition of graphene improved the electrical conductivity, benefiting the access of electrolyte and the transport of Na ions.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows the SEM image of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO after 100 cycles at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It is easy to see that the morphology and structure of the 12 aspects of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO are well maintained, and its size is measured to be about 700 nm, which is almost equal to the original size (∼730 nm). This fact clearly indicates that Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO may well mitigate large volume change of Co<sub>2</sub>P anode. Therefore, the designed Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO delivers a good electrochemical performance. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> shows the comparison of the rate performance of the metal phosphide materials reported before and in this work. In spite of a lower initial capacity, Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO is the most stable at a high current density, which shows a high rate performance.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO nanocomposites are successfully designed as anode materials for SIBs. ZIF-67 with a high porosity and a large surface area is utilized as the precursor. Moreover, the rGO is uniformly coated on the surface of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C to relieve volume changes. As a result, the Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO electrodes exhibit a high Na-storage capacity of 336 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle, as well as a good cyclability with 225 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles and a remarkable rate capability with 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a high rate of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles. It is believed that Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO show some potential as anode material for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b00444" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b00444</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Typical SEM image of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C; elemental mapping images and XPS atomic analyses of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO; cyclic voltammograms and discharge/charge voltage profiles of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C; Nyquist plots and typical fitted parameters of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C and Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO; SEM images of Co<sub>2</sub>P@N-C@rGO after 100 cycles; comparison with the previously reported results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b00444/suppl_file/am8b00444_si_001.pdf">am8b00444_si_001.pdf (1.0 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b00444" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review of advanced and practical lithium battery materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Marom, Rotem; Amalraj, S. Francis; Leifer, Nicole; Jacob, David; Aurbach, Doron</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9938-9954</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Presented herein is a discussion of the forefront in research and development of advanced electrode materials and electrolyte solns. for the next generation of lithium ion batteries.  The main challenge of the field today is in meeting the demands necessary to make the elec. vehicle fully com. viable.  This requires high energy and power densities with no compromise in safety.  Three families of advanced cathode materials (the limiting factor for energy d. in the Li battery systems) are discussed in detail: LiMn1.5Ni0.5O4 high voltage spinel compds., Li2MnO3-LiMO2 high capacity composite layered compds., and LiMPO4, where M = Fe, Mn.  Graphite, Si, LixTOy, and MO (conversion reactions) are discussed as anode materials.  The electrolyte is a key component that dets. the ability to use high voltage cathodes and low voltage anodes in the same system.  Electrode-soln. interactions and passivation phenomena on both electrodes in Li-ion batteries also play significant roles in detg. stability, cycle life and safety features.  This presentation is aimed at providing an overall picture of the road map necessary for the future development of advanced high energy d. Li-ion batteries for EV applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrHG44yQ70js7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liAf1Snl-AOTQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXotFSnurc%253D&amp;md5=080369f3bd98476b8bd50a778dae733f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lawes, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Controlled SnO<sub>2</sub> crystallinity effectively dominating sodium storage performance</span>. <i>Adv.                             cas_R

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Update on anode materials for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Hongyan; Liu, Yongchang; Cao, Kangzhe; Zhao, Yan; Jiao, Lifang; Wang, Yijing; Yuan, Huatang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
